<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Phased diploid genome assemblies and pan-genomes provide insights into the genetic history of apple domestication"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2020 52:12"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-11-02"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 This is a U.S. government work and not under copyright protection in the U.S.; foreign copyright protection may apply"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Domestication of the apple was mainly driven by interspecific hybridization. In the present study, we report the haplotype-resolved genomes of the cultivated apple (Malus domestica cv. Gala) and its two major wild progenitors, M. sieversii and M. sylvestris. Substantial variations are identified between the two haplotypes of each genome. Inference of genome ancestry identifies ~23% of the Gala genome as of hybrid origin. Deep sequencing of 91 accessions identifies selective sweeps in cultivated apples that originated from either of the two progenitors and are associated with important domestication traits. Construction and analyses of apple pan-genomes uncover thousands of new genes, with hundreds of them being selected from one of the progenitors and largely fixed in cultivated apples, revealing that introgression of new genes/alleles is a hallmark of apple domestication through hybridization. Finally, transcriptome profiles of Gala fruits at 13 developmental stages unravel ~19% of genes displaying allele-specific expression, including many associated with fruit quality. Phased diploid genomes of the cultivated apple Malus domestica cv. Gala and its two major wild progenitors M. sieversii and M. sylvestris, as well as pan-genome analyses, provide insights into the genetic history of apple domestication."/>

            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-0443-0406"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-0443-0406</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff3">3</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Shandong Academy of Agricultural Sciences" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.452757.6, 0000 0004 0644 6150, Shandong Centre of Crop Germplasm Resources, Shandong Academy of Agricultural Sciences, Jinan, Shandong, China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Awais-Khan" data-author-popup="auth-Awais-Khan">Awais Khan</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff4">4</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Cornell University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.5386.8, 000000041936877X, Plant Pathology and Plant-Microbe Biology Section, School of Integrative Plant Science, Cornell University, Geneva, NY, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Seunghyun-Ban" data-author-popup="auth-Seunghyun-Ban">Seunghyun Ban</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-8239-8179"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-8239-8179</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Cornell University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.5386.8, 000000041936877X, Horticulture Section, School of Integrative Plant Science, New York State Agricultural Experiment Station, Cornell University, Geneva, NY, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Kenong-Xu" data-author-popup="auth-Kenong-Xu">Kenong Xu</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-5254-5350"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-5254-5350</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Cornell University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.5386.8, 000000041936877X, Horticulture Section, School of Integrative Plant Science, New York State Agricultural Experiment Station, Cornell University, Geneva, NY, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Lailiang-Cheng" data-author-popup="auth-Lailiang-Cheng">Lailiang Cheng</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff6">6</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Cornell University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.5386.8, 000000041936877X, Horticulture Section, School of Integrative Plant Science, Cornell University, Ithaca, NY, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Gan_Yuan-Zhong" data-author-popup="auth-Gan_Yuan-Zhong" data-corresp-id="c1">Gan-Yuan Zhong<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-6499-3337"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-6499-3337</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Plant Genetic Resources Unit" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.507316.6, US Department of Agriculture, Agricultural Research Service, Plant Genetic Resources Unit, Geneva, NY, USA" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Zhangjun-Fei" data-author-popup="auth-Zhangjun-Fei" data-corresp-id="c2">Zhangjun Fei<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-9684-1450"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-9684-1450</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a>,<a href="#Aff7">7</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Boyce Thompson Institute" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.5386.8, 000000041936877X, Boyce Thompson Institute, Ithaca, NY, USA" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Robert W. Holley Center for Agriculture and Health" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.507316.6, US Department of Agriculture, Agricultural Research Service, Robert W. Holley Center for Agriculture and Health, Ithaca, NY, USA" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Domestication of the apple was mainly driven by interspecific hybridization. In the present study, we report the haplotype-resolved genomes of the cultivated apple (<i>Malus domestica</i> cv. Gala) and its two major wild progenitors, <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i>. Substantial variations are identified between the two haplotypes of each genome. Inference of genome ancestry identifies ~23% of the Gala genome as of hybrid origin. Deep sequencing of 91 accessions identifies selective sweeps in cultivated apples that originated from either of the two progenitors and are associated with important domestication traits. Construction and analyses of apple pan-genomes uncover thousands of new genes, with hundreds of them being selected from one of the progenitors and largely fixed in cultivated apples, revealing that introgression of new genes/alleles is a hallmark of apple domestication through hybridization. Finally, transcriptome profiles of Gala fruits at 13 developmental stages unravel ~19% of genes displaying allele-specific expression, including many associated with fruit quality.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Crop domestication has played a crucial role in human population expansion and civilization. Today, humans rely heavily on a number of crops that were domesticated thousands of years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Ross-Ibarra, J., Morrell, P. L. &amp; Gaut, B. S. Plant domestication, a unique opportunity to identify the genetic basis of adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 104, 8641–8648 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d38521e645">1</a></sup>. Genetic improvement of key crops for enhanced traits has been empowered by technical innovations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Shan, Q. et al. Targeted genome modification of crop plants using a CRISPR-Cas system. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 686–688 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d38521e649">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Hickey, L. T. et al. Breeding crops to feed 10 billion. Nat. Biotechnol. 37, 744–754 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d38521e652">3</a></sup>, but hindered by the narrow genetic diversity of the domesticated crops. Crop wild relatives represent an important source of genetic material for breeding, and genes underlying desirable traits in these wild relatives are often exploited to improve domesticated germplasm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Soyk, S. et al. Variation in the flowering gene SELF PRUNING 5G promotes day-neutrality and early yield in tomato. Nat. Genet. 49, 162–168 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d38521e656">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Tian, J. et al. Teosinte ligule allele narrows plant architecture and enhances high-density maize yields. Science 365, 658–664 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d38521e659">5</a></sup>. Despite its importance, genomic information for crop wild relatives is scarce<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Brozynska, M., Furtado, A. &amp; Henry, R. J. Genomics of crop wild relatives: expanding the gene pool for crop improvement. Plant Biotechnol. J. 14, 1070–1085 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d38521e663">6</a></sup>.</p><p>Most crop plants have complex genomes characterized by large size, high heterozygosity level and polyploidy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Van de Peer, Y., Mizrachi, E. &amp; Marchal, K. The evolutionary significance of polyploidy. Nat. Rev. Genet. 18, 411–424 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d38521e670">7</a></sup>. Such complexity challenges plant genome assembly, for which additional efforts on reference selection are often required to achieve a good-quality genome, and in many cases a homozygous line with lower ploidy level is favored<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wu, S. et al. Genome sequences of two diploid wild relatives of cultivated sweetpotato reveal targets for genetic improvement. Nat. Commun. 9, 4580 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d38521e674">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099–1106 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d38521e677">9</a></sup>. However, many plants are open pollinated in nature, whereby heterozygous genomic regions can be major contributors to phenotypic variations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Schnable, P. S. &amp; Springer, N. M. Progress toward understanding heterosis in crop plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 64, 71–88 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d38521e681">10</a></sup>. Hence, direct sequencing of naturally heterozygous lines can provide deep views on their genetic complexity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Minio, A., Massonnet, M., Figueroa-Balderas, R., Castro, A. &amp; Cantu, D. Diploid genome assembly of the wine grape Carménère. G3 9, 1331–1337 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d38521e685">11</a></sup>. On the other hand, plants tend to be genetically structured, and a single reference genome can by no means represent a whole population. Therefore, generation of a sophisticated representation of population diversity in addition to a linear reference genome is of great interest. Variants of such representation, including gene-based<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Zhao, Q. et al. Pan-genome analysis highlights the extent of genomic variation in cultivated and wild rice. Nat. Genet. 50, 278–284 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d38521e689">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Gao, L. et al. The tomato pan-genome uncovers new genes and a rare allele regulating fruit flavor. Nat. Genet. 51, 1044–1051 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d38521e692">13</a></sup> or sequence-based<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Peter, J. et al. Genome evolution across 1,011 Saccharomyces cerevisiae isolates. Nature 556, 339–344 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d38521e697">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Rakocevic, G. et al. Fast and accurate genomic analyses using genome graphs. Nat. Genet. 51, 354–362 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d38521e700">15</a></sup> pan-genomes, have successfully captured hidden genetic diversity and facilitated the discovery of a genetic basis of important traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Yang, X., Lee, W. P., Ye, K. &amp; Lee, C. One reference genome is not enough. Genome Biol. 20, 104 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d38521e704">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Tao, Y., Zhao, X., Mace, E., Henry, R. &amp; Jordan, D. Exploring and exploiting pan-genomics for crop improvement. Mol. Plant 12, 156–169 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d38521e707">17</a></sup>.</p><p>The apple (<i>Malus domestica</i> Borkh.) is a popular temperate fruit and its domestication was driven by hybridization of different wild species and clonal propagation of genotypes with desirable traits. Among the wild species, <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i> are the major progenitors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cornille, A., Giraud, T., Smulders, M. J., Roldán-Ruiz, I. &amp; Gladieux, P. The domestication and evolutionary ecology of apples. Trends Genet. 30, 57–65 (2014)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d38521e723">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cornille, A. et al. New insight into the history of domesticated apple: secondary contribution of the European wild apple to the genome of cultivated varieties. PLoS Genet. 8, e1002703 (2012)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d38521e723_1">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Duan, N. et al. Genome re-sequencing reveals the history of apple and supports a two-stage model for fruit enlargement. Nat. Commun. 8, 249 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d38521e726">20</a></sup>. The apple genome is highly heterozygous, posing a major challenge for earlier genome assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Li, X. et al. Improved hybrid de novo genome assembly of domesticated apple (Malus × domestica). GigaScience 5, 35 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d38521e730">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d38521e733">22</a></sup>. Currently, reference-quality genome assemblies of cultivated apple are available for a double-haploid line GDDH13 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099–1106 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d38521e738">9</a></sup>), a triple-haploid HFTH1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Zhang, L. et al. A high-quality apple genome assembly reveals the association of a retrotransposon and red fruit colour. Nat. Commun. 10, 1494 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d38521e742">23</a></sup>) and a diploid cultivar ‘Gala Galaxy’<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Broggini, G. A. et al. Chromosome-scale de novo diploid assembly of the apple cultivar ‘Gala Galaxy’. Preprint at bioRxiv &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.04.25.058891&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2020)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d38521e746">24</a></sup>; for its wild relatives, only a draft genome of <i>M. baccata</i> is available<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Chen, X. et al. Sequencing of a wild apple (Malus baccata) genome unravels the differences between cultivated and wild apple species regarding disease resistance and cold tolerance. G3 9, 2051–2060 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d38521e753">25</a></sup>. In the present study, we assembled reference-grade, phased diploid genomes for the cultivated apple Gala, a top cultivar grown worldwide, and two major wild progenitors, <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i>. We directly sequenced the heterozygous lines and disclosed the diploid state of the genomes. We also constructed a pan-genome for each of the three <i>Malus</i> species based on 91 deeply resequenced accessions. The high-quality reference and pan-genomes enable a better understanding of the genetic basis of apple domestication, and provide valuable resources for future apple research and breeding.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and homologous chromosome construction</h3><p>We generated a total of 623–780× coverage of Illumina and 10x Genomics sequences and an additional 37–81× coverage of PacBio HiFi sequences for the three accessions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">1</a>). For each accession, reads were assembled into a diploid genome comprising phased scaffolds, as well as a conventional haploid consensus (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">1</a>). The final assemblies had sizes of 1.31–1.32 Gb for diploid genomes and 652–668 Mb for haploid consensuses (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">2</a>). Despite high heterozygosity rates (0.85–1.28%), all assemblies showed high contiguity, with the scaffold N50 of 3.3–4.3 Mb in diploid assemblies and 16.8–35.7 Mb in haploid consensuses (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">2</a>). Using high-density genetic maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Di Pierro, E. A. et al. A high-density, multi-parental SNP genetic map on apple validates a new mapping approach for outcrossing species. Hortic. Res. 3, 16057 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d38521e791">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Howard, N. P. et al. Elucidation of the ‘Honeycrisp’ pedigree through haplotype analysis with a multi-family integrated SNP linkage map and a large apple (Malus × domestica) pedigree-connected SNP data set. Hortic. Res. 4, 17003 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d38521e794">27</a></sup> and genomic synteny with the published assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099–1106 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d38521e799">9</a></sup>, 96.7–97.8% of scaffolds in haploid consensuses were successfully anchored.</p><p>The diploid assemblies were approximately twice the size of the haploid consensuses, indicating that the homologous chromosomes were well captured in each assembly, which was further supported by the <i>k</i>-mer spectrum analysis (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig7">1</a>). Approximately 93.7–95.5% of the phased scaffolds were separated into two nonredundant collections (a.k.a. haplomes), which were further anchored to 17 homologous chromosomes. The cumulative size of each haplome was 88.5–100.0% of the haploid consensus (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">2</a>), and all three accessions showed high levels of collinearity between the two haplomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig1">1a</a>). Genome evaluation using multiple approaches confirmed the high quality of both haploid and diploid assemblies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig8">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genome and evolution of Gala, M. sieversii and M. sylvestris."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Genome and evolution of Gala, <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-00723-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_723_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-00723-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_723_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="660" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Genomic synteny between homologous chromosomes (haplomes) of the diploid assemblies. Numbers indicate chromosomes. <b>b</b>, Evolutionary history and divergence time of the apple. The estimated divergence times (Ma) are inferred based on single-copy orthologous groups, and the 95% highest posterior density ranges (in the brackets) are shown at each node. The colored <i>M. domestica</i> represent the ancestral subpopulations of <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i> that are the direct genetic contributors of modern-day cultivated apples. WGD, whole genome duplication. <b>c</b>, Distribution of sequence divergence of intact LTR-RTs. Peak values were converted to ages based on a mutation rate of 3.9 × 10<sup>−9</sup> substitutions per site per year.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A total of 90,147–90,507 protein-coding genes were predicted in the diploid assemblies and 45,199–45,352 in the haploid assemblies (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">2</a>). The nucleotide-binding, leucine-rich repeat genes are broadly involved in disease resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Meng, D. et al. Sorbitol modulates resistance to Alternaria alternata by regulating the expression of an NLR resistance gene in apple. Plant Cell 30, 1562–1581 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d38521e878">28</a></sup>, and were found to be highly variable in the <i>Malus</i> spp. (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">3</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><p>Our assemblies showed an overall high collinearity with the published genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">3</a>), with an exception of a 5-Mb inversion on chromosome 1, which we found was probably a mis-assembly in both GDDH13 and HFTH1 genomes (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig9">3</a>). We identified substantial variations between the haplomes, including 2,387,290, 2,591,444 and 2,929,832 single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs), 363,464, 364,605 and 401,893 insertions/deletions, and 202, 343 and 330 inversions in <i>M. sieversii</i>, <i>M. sylvestris</i> and Gala, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">4</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">4</a>).</p><p>Approximately 58.7–59.4% of the three apple genomes were repeat sequences, similar to those in genomes of GDDH13 and HFTH1 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">5</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">5</a>). We found that two long terminal repeat retrotransposon (LTR-RT) bursts occurred during apple evolution, with the older one taking place before speciation of apple and pear<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Linsmith, G. et al. Pseudo-chromosome-length genome assembly of a double haploid ‘Bartlett’ pear (Pyrus communis L.). GigaScience 8, giz138 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d38521e927">29</a></sup> and the recent one happening before the time when <i>M. sylvestris</i> and <i>M. sieversii</i> diversified into subpopulations, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig1">1b,c</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">7</a>). Evolution of LTR-RTs after repeated bursts may have created abundant genetic diversity among species. A notable example is the redTE retrotransposon, which translocated into the <i>MYB1</i> locus in some apple cultivars that caused red fruit skin color<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Zhang, L. et al. A high-quality apple genome assembly reveals the association of a retrotransposon and red fruit colour. Nat. Commun. 10, 1494 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d38521e950">23</a></sup>. We found that redTE was present only in <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. domestica</i>, and its presence as a short and heterozygous form in Gala probably caused <i>MYB1</i> allele-specific expression and consequently the yellowish-red fruit skin color of Gala (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig10">4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genomic origin of the cultivated apple</h3><p>Syntenic regions of <i>M</i>. <i>sieversii</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>sylvestris</i>, with respect to the Gala genome, were genetically divergent and formed mutually distinguishable blocks where the boundaries of the discrete blocks indicated the possible recombination sites in Gala (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig11">5</a>). The genetic distance between the two haplomes in Gala fluctuated across chromosomes and the average divergence was higher than that in the two wild species (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig12">6</a>). We found that the highly divergent regions between the two Gala haplomes corresponded to a hybrid origin of the two alleles, whereas less divergent regions underlined homozygous alleles that originated from either <i>M. sieversii</i> or <i>M. sylvestris</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig2">2a</a>). We inferred that the portion of cultivated apple genomes probably derived from <i>M. sieversii</i> ranged from 28% to 40%, and from <i>M. sylvestris</i> 25–37% (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig2">2b</a>). The heterozygous Gala genome encoded 23% of the sequence with hybrid ancestry, indicating that a considerable portion of the Gala genome has preserved genetic information from both progenitors (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig2">2b</a>). Moreover, 28% of syntenic regions among the three cultivated apples were derived from different progenitors (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig13">7</a>), suggesting frequent recombination in apple genomes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genetic contribution of wild progenitors to the cultivated apple."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Genetic contribution of wild progenitors to the cultivated apple.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-00723-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_723_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-00723-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_723_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="810" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Genetic distance between the two haplomes of the diploid assemblies (blue lines in the upper three panels), and between Gala haplomes and the consensus assemblies of either <i>M. sieversii</i> (green lines in the lower two panels) or <i>M. sylvestris</i> (purple lines). The highlighted areas indicate genomic regions with elevated divergence between Gala haplomes. Chromosome 15 (Chr15) is shown here whereas other chromosomes are shown in Extended Data Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig12">6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig13">7</a>. <b>b</b>, Summary of genomic contributions of the two wild progenitors to Gala, GDDH13 and HFTH1. <b>c</b>, Neighbor-joining phylogeny of 91 apple accessions based on 9,988,777 biallelic SNPs of the nuclear genome. Colored dots on cultivated apple branches indicate the origin of mitochondrial genomes. <b>d</b>, Proportion of genomes of the cultivated apple that originated from the two wild progenitors. Colored dots indicating the origin of mitochondrial genomes are the same as in <b>c</b>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To make a population-scale inference, we collected 91 accessions (43 <i>M. domestica</i>, 37 <i>M. sieversii</i> and 11 <i>M. sylvestris</i>), including 71 from our previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Duan, N. et al. Genome re-sequencing reveals the history of apple and supports a two-stage model for fruit enlargement. Nat. Commun. 8, 249 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1081">20</a></sup>. Of these 91 accessions, 59 were further or newly deep sequenced, yielding a total of 2.7 Tb data with an average depth of 59.3× (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">6</a>). A total of 9,988,777 biallelic SNPs and 323,047 structure variants (SVs) were identified. Phylogenetic and principal component analyses indicated that <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i> accessions clustered into clearly separated monophyletic clades, whereas <i>M. domestica</i> accessions clustered within paraphyletic groups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig2">2c</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">8</a>). Estimation of genomic origin in these accessions revealed substantial genetic contribution of the two wild progenitors to the cultivated apple (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig2">2d</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">7</a>). Phylogeny based on the assembled mitochondrial or chloroplast genomes showed that 30 of the 43 <i>M. domestica</i> accessions clustered with <i>M. sylvestris</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">9</a>), indicating substantial maternal pedigree from <i>M. sylvestris</i> in the cultivated apple.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Selective sweeps underlying trait inheritance from different progenitors</h3><p>The cultivated apple bears many traits that are selected during domestication. Some traits, including fruit weight, firmness, sweetness and tartness, resemble either of the two progenitors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Duan, N. et al. Genome re-sequencing reveals the history of apple and supports a two-stage model for fruit enlargement. Nat. Commun. 8, 249 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1131">20</a></sup>. We identified a total of 1,633 and 1,504 genomic regions under selection for the comparisons of <i>M. domestica</i> with <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i>, respectively, with cumulative sizes of 18.5 and 18.9 Mb, and harboring 1,400 and 1,259 genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig3">3a,b</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">9</a>). Among the 3,137 selective regions, 1,295 and 1,121 shared genome ancestry with <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i>, respectively. The genomic region with the highest selection score (XP-CLR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Chen, H., Patterson, N. &amp; Reich, D. Population differentiation as a test for selective sweeps. Genome Res. 20, 393–402 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1160">30</a></sup>) contained nine genes on chromosome 6 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig3">3c</a>), including one CLAVATA3/ESR (CLE)-related gene, which was upregulated during apple fruit development (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig3">3d</a>), and one encoding a short-chain dehydrogenase/reductase (SDR). The CLE family comprises a major group of signaling peptides and displays diverse functions in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Hirakawa, Y. &amp; Sawa, S. Diverse function of plant peptide hormones in local signaling and development. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 51, 81–87 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1171">31</a></sup>. A notable example is <i>CLV3</i>, the loss-of-function alleles of which can increase tomato yield by regulating fruit size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Rodríguez-Leal, D., Lemmon, Z. H., Man, J., Bartlett, M. E. &amp; Lippman, Z. B. Engineering quantitative trait variation for crop improvement by genome editing. Cell 171, 470–480 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1178">32</a></sup>. SDR is involved in the production of alcohol-related substrates, which are important compounds contributing to apple fruit aroma<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Espino-Díaz, M., Sepúlveda, D. R., González-Aguilar, G. &amp; Olivas, G. I. Biochemistry of apple aroma: a review. Food Technol. Biotechnol. 54, 375–394 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1182">33</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Selective sweeps in the cultivated apple genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Selective sweeps in the cultivated apple genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-00723-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_723_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-00723-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_723_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="840" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>,<b>b</b>, Selective sweeps and candidate genes in cultivated apple compared with <i>M. sieversii</i> (<b>a</b>) and <i>M. sylvestris</i> (<b>b</b>). Chromosomes are painted with either dark-blue or orange, whereas the gray points indicate genomic regions that originated from the other wild progenitor rather than the compared one. Green lines define the top 1% XP-CLR scores. Genes inside boxes were differentially expressed during apple fruit development. <b>c</b>, SNP genotype, nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) and Tajima’s <i>D</i> in the specified genome region of the three <i>Malus</i> populations. <b>d</b>, Expression profiles of selected genes during apple fruit development. The mean of FPKM (fragments per kilobase of exon per million mapped fragments) and its s.e. derived from three biological replicates are shown. <b>e</b>, Genetic distance showing the origin of the selected chromosomes from the two wild progenitors. Shade in chromosome 6 indicates the region originated from <i>M. sieversii</i>, which is surrounded by recombination spots indicated by blue arrows.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The cultivated apple inherited its fruit size mainly from <i>M. sieversii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Cornille, A., Giraud, T., Smulders, M. J., Roldán-Ruiz, I. &amp; Gladieux, P. The domestication and evolutionary ecology of apples. Trends Genet. 30, 57–65 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1251">18</a></sup>. A fruit weight quantitative trait locus (QTL)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Liu, Z. et al. Construction of a genetic linkage map and QTL analysis of fruit-related traits in an F1 Red Fuji × Hongrou apple hybrid. Open Life Sci. 11, 487–497 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1255">34</a></sup> on chromosome 6 (24–29 Mb) was found in a region surrounded by recombination spots, whereas only the <i>M. sieversii</i> allele was inherited by the cultivated apple (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig3">3e</a>), suggesting a strong selection on this region during domestication. A selective sweep containing a tryptophan aminotransferase-related (<i>TAR</i>) gene was found within this QTL (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig3">3a,e</a>). <i>TAR</i> is involved in indole-3-acetic acid biosynthesis and its expression was high at early fruit developmental stages (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig3">3d</a>). It is of interest that overexpression of the <i>TAR</i> homolog in wheat enhanced grain yield<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Shao, A. et al. The auxin biosynthetic TRYPTOPHAN AMINOTRANSFERASE RELATED TaTAR2.1-3A increases grain yield of wheat. Plant Physiol. 174, 2274–2288 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1281">35</a></sup>, indicating a potential role of <i>TAR</i> in apple fruit size regulation.</p><p>Wild and cultivated apples show a remarkable difference in fruit acidity. The <i>Ma1</i> gene encoding a malate transporter was identified as a major locus controlling apple fruit acidity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Bai, Y. et al. A natural mutation-led truncation in one of the two aluminum-activated malate transporter-like genes at the Ma locus is associated with low fruit acidity in apple. Mol. Genet. Genom. 287, 663–678 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1294">36</a></sup>. A mutation (G to A) at base 1,455 of the <i>Ma1</i> coding sequence leads to protein truncation that is largely responsible for the low fruit acidity in apples<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Li, C. L. et al. Apple ALMT9 requires a conserved C-terminal domain for malate transport underlying fruit acidity. Plant Physiol. 182, 992–1006 (2020)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1301">37</a></sup>. We found that the frequency of the ‘A’ allele was low in <i>M. sylvestris</i> (4.5%), but dramatically increased in <i>M. sieversii</i> (33.3%) and the cultivated apple (55.6%), consistent with fruit acidity levels of the cultivated apple being comparable to <i>M. sieversii</i> but substantially lower than <i>M. sylvestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Duan, N. et al. Genome re-sequencing reveals the history of apple and supports a two-stage model for fruit enlargement. Nat. Commun. 8, 249 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1317">20</a></sup>. These data suggest that the low acidity ‘A’ allele of the cultivated apple is inherited mainly from <i>M. sieversii</i>, as is the case in Gala (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig3">3e</a>). Moreover, a much higher proportion of the cultivated apple (66.7%) harbored both alleles than <i>M. sieversii</i> (44.4%) and <i>M. sylvestris</i> (9.1%), consistent with a heterozygous locus in cultivars being responsible for the moderate acidity favored by consumers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Bai, Y. et al. A natural mutation-led truncation in one of the two aluminum-activated malate transporter-like genes at the Ma locus is associated with low fruit acidity in apple. Mol. Genet. Genom. 287, 663–678 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1334">36</a></sup>.</p><p>The firm texture of cultivated apple fruits is mainly contributed by <i>M. sylvestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Cornille, A., Giraud, T., Smulders, M. J., Roldán-Ruiz, I. &amp; Gladieux, P. The domestication and evolutionary ecology of apples. Trends Genet. 30, 57–65 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1343">18</a></sup>. The apple <i>PG1</i> (polygalacturonase 1) gene harbors two different alleles with one associated with crispy texture and the other with mealy texture<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Longhi, S. et al. A candidate gene based approach validates Md-PG1 as the main responsible for a QTL impacting fruit texture in apple (Malus × domestica Borkh). BMC Plant Biol. 13, 37 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1350">38</a></sup>. Most <i>M. sieversii</i> accessions harbored the homozygous mealy texture-associated allele, whereas <i>M. sylvestris</i> accessions mainly comprised the homozygous crispy texture-associated allele (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">10</a>). Cultivated apples harbored a much higher percentage of heterozygous genotype of <i>PG1</i>, suggesting a favorable fruit firmness achieved through hybridization of wild progenitors during domestication. In addition, a nonsynonymous SNP (C/A) in the transcription factor gene <i>NAC18.1</i> was found to be associated with fruit firmness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Yeats, T. H. et al. Allelic diversity of NAC18.1 is a major determinant of fruit firmness and harvest date in apple (Malus domestica). Preprint at bioRxiv &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/708040&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1370">39</a></sup>. Overexpression of the <i>NAC18.1</i> haplotype containing the ‘C’ allele resulted in firmer fruit than that with the ‘A’ allele<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Yeats, T. H. et al. Allelic diversity of NAC18.1 is a major determinant of fruit firmness and harvest date in apple (Malus domestica). Preprint at bioRxiv &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/708040&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1377">39</a></sup>. We found that the ‘C’ allele was fixed in <i>M. sylvestris</i> (100%) and predominant (82.0%) in the cultivated apple, whereas in <i>M. sieversii</i> the ‘A’ allele was predominant (67.0%), consistent with the genomic region harboring <i>NAC18.1</i> being derived from <i>M. sylvestris</i> in all three cultivars (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig3">3e</a>). Moreover, the major blue mold resistance QTL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Norelli, J. L. et al. Genotyping-by-sequencing markers facilitate the identification of quantitative trait loci controlling resistance to Penicillium expansum in Malus sieversii. PLoS ONE 12, e0172949 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1397">40</a></sup>, which was also derived from <i>M. sylvestris</i>, co-localized with this fruit firmness locus (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig3">3e</a>). Many <i>M. sieversii</i> accessions are resistant to blue mold, whereas most cultivars are susceptible, indicating a possible hitchhiking effect caused by fruit firmness selection in the cultivated apple during domestication.</p><p>Gala, GDDH13 and HFTH1 all inherited chromosome 17 largely from <i>M. sylvestris</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig3">3e</a>). This chromosome harbored selective sweeps overlapping with QTLs associated with apple polyphenol content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Chagné, D. et al. QTL and candidate gene mapping for polyphenolic composition in apple fruit. BMC Plant Biol. 12, 12 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1421">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Verdu, C. F. et al. QTL analysis and candidate gene mapping for the polyphenol content in cider apple. PLoS ONE 9, e107103 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1424">42</a></sup> and containing genes related to cell-wall biosynthesis, including two encoding cellulose synthases (CLSs). One CLS gene and several other cell-wall-related genes in sweep regions of other chromosomes (for example, pectin acetylesterase (PAE), xyloglucan 6-xylosyltransferase (XXT) and xyloglucan endotransglucosylase/hydrolase (XTH)) were all upregulated during fruit development (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig3">3a,d</a>), therefore probably contributing to apple fruit firmness. In addition, we identified sweeps that contained genes associated with fruit sweetness, including those encoding sugar/sucrose transporter (ST/SUT), sucrose synthase (SS) and β-glucosidase/galactosidase (BG/BGL) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig3">3a,b,d</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Population structure and demographic history of apples</h3><p>Population structure analysis indicated two obvious gene pools in apple cultivars (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig4">4a</a>). The <i>M. sieversii</i> accessions were collected from Kazakhstan and Xinjiang, China, two sides of the Tian Shan mountains. Under the optimal number of clusters (<i>K</i> = 3; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">11</a>), the Xinjiang accessions showed homogeneous genetic background with low levels of introgression from other populations. In contrast, the Kazakhstan accessions consisted of two gene pools and some accessions were under intensive introgression (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig4">4a</a>). Accessions with high levels of introgression were sampled in places closer to the route of the ancient Silk Road (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">12</a>), suggesting that massive introgression may have occurred during westward dissemination of the apple along the Silk Road<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Cornille, A., Giraud, T., Smulders, M. J., Roldán-Ruiz, I. &amp; Gladieux, P. The domestication and evolutionary ecology of apples. Trends Genet. 30, 57–65 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1461">18</a></sup>. <i>M. sylvestris</i> comprised three geological subpopulations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig4">4a</a>), two of which are known to be from south-east Europe and western Europe. Introgressions between these subpopulations were rare, confirming geographic isolation among subpopulations.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Population structure and demographic history of the apple."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Population structure and demographic history of the apple.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-00723-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_723_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-00723-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_723_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="369" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Population structure of the cultivated apple and the two wild progenitors, <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i>. Only clusters (<i>K</i>) with the highest <i>ΔK</i> are shown. <b>b</b>, Effective population size of <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i>. Colored lines correspond to accessions labeled with the same colors as the asterisks in <b>a</b>. Highlighted areas represent the time interval of the PGP and the LGM.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Inference on demographic history indicated a decline of effective population size (<i>N</i><sub>e</sub>) starting ~0.9 million years ago (Ma) for both <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig4">4b</a>). The contraction of <i>N</i><sub>e</sub> occurred during the ice age known as quaternary glaciation (2.58 Ma to the present), and was common in many other plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, L. et al. Genome of wild mandarin and domestication history of mandarin. Mol. Plant 11, 1024–1037 (2018)." href="#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1539">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhou, Y., Massonnet, M., Sanjak, J. S., Cantu, D. &amp; Gaut, B. S. Evolutionary genomics of grape (Vitis vinifera ssp. vinifera) domestication. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 114, 11715–11720 (2017)." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1539_1">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Yu, Y. et al. Genome re-sequencing reveals the evolutionary history of peach fruit edibility. Nat. Commun. 9, 5404 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1542">45</a></sup>. The <i>N</i><sub>e</sub> of <i>M. sieversii</i> reached the bottom and then started to rebound 128 to 123 thousand years ago (ka), which corresponded to the termination of the penultimate glacial period (PGP) (130 to 113 ka) and the onset of the last interglacial period (130 to 115 ka), during which deglaciation took place<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Petit, J.-R. et al. Climate and atmospheric history of the past 420,000 years from the Vostok ice core, Antarctica. Nature 399, 429–436 (1999)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1553">46</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig4">4b</a>). The second bottleneck for <i>M. sieversii</i> started 40 to 30 ka, right before the onset of the last glacial maximum (LGM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Clark, P. U. et al. The last glacial maximum. Science 325, 710–714 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1564">47</a></sup> (33 to 19 ka) which caused an inhospitable environment for living organisms. The <i>M. sylvestris</i> population continued to decline in the long and distant past, indicating that climate fluctuation may have led to continuous population contraction of <i>M. sylvestris</i>. This coincides with the hypothesis that quaternary glaciation has narrowed down and fragmented the geological range of <i>M. sylvestris</i>, therefore leading to the emergence of geographic subpopulations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Cornille, A. et al. Postglacial recolonization history of the European crabapple (Malus sylvestris Mill.), a wild contributor to the domesticated apple. Mol. Ecol. 22, 2249–2263 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1578">48</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Pan-genomes of cultivated and wild apples</h3><p>We constructed pan-genomes for the three <i>Malus</i> species separately by de novo assembly of the resequencing data for each accession (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">13</a>). Consequently, a total of 89-, 212- and 141-Mb nonredundant, nonreference sequences harboring 1,736, 3,438 and 2,104 new genes were identified for <i>M. sylvestris</i>, <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. domestica</i>, respectively, which brought pan-genomes containing 46,935, 48,648 and 49,944 protein-coding genes. Modeling of pan-genome sizes suggested closed/saturated pan-genomes for all three species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig5">5a</a>). Gene Ontology term enrichment analysis on the new genes suggested that genes associated with pollination, signal transduction and response to stress were highly enriched (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">14</a>). <i>M. domestica</i> had the largest pan-genome due to genetic introgression from wild species during domestication, whereas <i>M. sylvestris</i> had the smallest, congruent with the constant bottleneck in its demographic history (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig4">4b</a>). The fraction of core genes in the three pan-genomes (81.3–87.3%; Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig14">8a</a>) was higher than that in the annual plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Gao, L. et al. The tomato pan-genome uncovers new genes and a rare allele regulating fruit flavor. Nat. Genet. 51, 1044–1051 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1626">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Contreras-Moreira, B. et al. Analysis of plant pan-genomes and transcriptomes with GET_HOMOLOGUES-EST, a clustering solution for sequences of the same species. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 184 (2017)." href="#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1629">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hurgobin, B. et al. Homoeologous exchange is a major cause of gene presence/absence variation in the amphidiploid Brassica napus. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 1265–1274 (2018)." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1629_1">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Golicz, A. A. et al. The pangenome of an agronomically important crop plant Brassica oleracea. Nat. Commun. 7, 13390 (2016)." href="#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1629_2">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Montenegro, J. D. et al. The pangenome of hexaploid bread wheat. Plant J. 90, 1007–1013 (2017)." href="#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1629_3">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, W. et al. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature 557, 43–49 (2018)." href="#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1629_4">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, Y.-h et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045–1052 (2014)." href="#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1629_5">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Gordon, S. P. et al. Extensive gene content variation in the Brachypodium distachyon pan-genome correlates with population structure. Nat. Commun. 8, 2184 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1632">55</a></sup> (35–81%). The core genomes of the three species showed a different trend, with <i>M. domestica</i> having the lowest fraction of core genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig5">5b</a>). Core genes were relatively conserved across species, whereas variable genes showed high plasticity in different species (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig14">8b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Pan-genomes of the cultivated apple and the two wild progenitor species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Pan-genomes of the cultivated apple and the two wild progenitor species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-00723-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_723_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-00723-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_723_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="651" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>,<b>b</b>, Modeling of pan-genomes (<b>a</b>) and core genomes (<b>b</b>) of the three <i>Malus</i> species. The size of pan-genome or core genome at each specified number of accessions was modeled with power–law distribution. The solid line shows fitting to the maximum number of sampled accessions, whereas the dashed line indicates the exploration of the fitting. <b>c</b>, PAV of pan-genome orthologues in <i>M. domestica</i>, <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i>. Only orthologues containing genes absent in at least one accession were shown. Dom, <i>M. domestica</i>; Sie, <i>M. sieversii</i>; Syl, <i>M. sylvestris</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The presence/absence variation (PAV) pattern of pan-genomes can serve as an indicator of genes under selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Gao, L. et al. The tomato pan-genome uncovers new genes and a rare allele regulating fruit flavor. Nat. Genet. 51, 1044–1051 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1704">13</a></sup>. To identify cross-species PAVs, we clustered the three pan-genomes into 69,411 orthologues (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">11</a>). The phylogeny of the apple accessions inferred using PAVs (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">15</a>) was consistent with the SNP phylogeny (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig2">2c</a>). We identified 851 orthologues that were favored in both <i>M. domestica</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i> compared with <i>M. sieversii</i>, 316 favored in both <i>M. domestica</i> and <i>M. sieversii</i> compared with <i>M. sylvestris</i>, and 17 exclusively favored in <i>M. domestica</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig5">5c</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">12</a>).
