The much fewer uniquely favored genes in <i>M. domestica</i> than those shared with one of the wild progenitors suggests that many trait-associated genes in the cultivated apple may have already been functionally divergent in the wild progenitors, whereas domestication may have created relatively few innovations on the selection of genes. PAVs of disease resistance genes as well as self-incompatibility genes were found in different groups of favored genes, congruent with their rapid evolution. Several cell-wall biogenesis-associated genes were favored in both <i>M. sylvestris</i> and <i>M. domestica</i> compared with <i>M. sieversii</i>, whereas genes encoding a chitinase and three WRKY transcription factors, with <i>Arabidopsis</i> homologs that conferred drought tolerance, were favored in both <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. domestica</i> when compared with <i>M. sylvestris</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">12</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Widespread ASE during apple fruit development</h3><p>The phased diploid genomes allowed investigation of allele-specific expression (ASE) at a high resolution. We performed transcriptome profiling of Gala fruits at 13 different stages throughout fruit development (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">13</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig15">9</a>). Principal component analysis showed that the fruit transcriptomes were mainly shaped by genome-wide ASE, and secondarily by expression of genes at different developmental stages (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig6">6a</a>). We found that 8,569 (19% of total) genes showed ASE in Gala fruits, with 79% of them having allele imbalance at multiple stages (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">14</a>). Most ASE genes had a dominant allele with expression that was consistently higher than that of the other allele at all stages showing ASE, whereas switch of allele dominance was observed for only 112 genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig6">6b</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig16">10</a>). Among the ASE genes, those with two alleles derived from different progenitors were overrepresented (Fisher’s exact test; <i>P</i> = 9.4 × 10<sup>−49</sup>); however, expression of different types of ASE genes classified based on their allele ancestry did not show bias toward either progenitor (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig6">6b</a>), reinforcing the critical contribution of both progenitors in gene expression regulation in cultivated apples. We found that SVs between the two Gala haplomes located upstream of genes were significantly closer to ASE genes than to other genes (Mann–Whitney <i>U</i>-test; <i>P</i> = 4.5 × 10<sup>− 23</sup>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">16a</a>), suggesting that SVs could function as <i>cis</i> variations that drive ASE in Gala.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Allele-specific gene expression in Gala fruit."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Allele-specific gene expression in Gala fruit.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-00723-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_723_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-00723-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_723_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="714" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Principal component (PC) analysis of Gala fruit allele expression profiles at 13 different developmental stages. <b>b</b>, Allele expression pattern of genes with defined allele ancestry and showing ASE in at least five fruit stages. Sie, <i>M. sieversii</i>; Syl, <i>M. sylvestris</i>. <b>c</b>, Expression of the <i>PG1</i> two alleles. <b>d</b>,<b>e</b>, Expression (<b>d</b>) and structure variation (<b>e</b>) of the two alleles of <i>AAT1</i> in Gala. <b>f</b>, Expression of the two alleles of the gene encoding a UGT.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Many of the ASE genes were associated with fruit development and quality, including those encoding ACC oxidases and RIN-like MADS-box transcription factor for fruit ripening, OVATE family proteins for fruit shape, cell-wall metabolic genes involved in fruit texture, and genes associated with biosynthesis of phytohormones, flavonoids and aroma volatiles (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">16b</a>). The Gala genome harbored both <i>PG1</i> alleles, the expression of which was low and indistinguishable at most of the fruit developmental stages. However, at the ripening stage (127 d after full-bloom (d.a.f.)), the mealy texture-associated allele was expressed to a much higher level (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig6">6c</a>), consistent with the relatively soft fruit texture of Gala. The aroma of ripe apples arises from a complex mixture of volatile compounds including esters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Espino-Díaz, M., Sepúlveda, D. R., González-Aguilar, G. &amp; Olivas, G. I. Biochemistry of apple aroma: a review. Food Technol. Biotechnol. 54, 375–394 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1895">33</a></sup>. The apple <i>AAT1</i> encoding an alcohol acyl transferase is the major gene controlling ester production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Souleyre, E. J. et al. The AAT1 locus is critical for the biosynthesis of esters contributing to ‘ripe apple’ flavour in ‘Royal Gala’ and ‘Granny Smith’ apples. Plant J. 78, 903–915 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1903">56</a></sup>. The <i>AAT1</i> gene in Gala displayed strong ASE, with the <i>M. sieversii</i>-derived allele expressed at much lower levels than the <i>M. sylvestris</i>-originated allele at most developmental stages (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig6">6d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">16c</a>). We identified an insertion of a 509-bp miniature inverted-repeat transposable element–like sequence in the upstream region of the <i>M. sieversii</i>-derived allele (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig6">6e</a>). Such insertion was also found in the cultivar ‘Granny Smith’ that harbors homozygous alleles and produces low levels of ester, demonstrating its association with <i>AAT1</i> expression and ester production, and suggesting that Gala ester production is mainly attributed to the <i>M. sylvestris</i>-originated allele. Flavor-related volatiles in developing fruits can be modified by UDP-glycosyltransferases (UGTs), which are also involved in the biosynthesis of anthocyanin and flavonols<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Song, C. et al. A UDP‐glucosyltransferase functions in both acylphloroglucinol glucoside and anthocyanin biosynthesis in strawberry (Fragaria × ananassa). Plant J. 85, 730–742 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1935">57</a></sup>. Among the 69 UGTs showing ASE in Gala fruits, 30 were dominated by the allele originating from <i>M. sieversii</i>, whereas 18 were dominated by the <i>M. sylvestris</i>-derived allele. Unlike other UGTs with one allele dominant throughout fruit development, the two alleles of the UGT91 subfamily gene <i>Mdg_10g010160</i> showed distinct dominant patterns. The <i>M. sieversii</i>-originated allele dominated at the early developmental stages whereas the <i>M. sylvestris</i>-derived allele dominated during ripening (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig6">6f</a>). As the transcriptional regulation of fruit development and ripening is tightly controlled<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Giovannoni, J., Nguyen, C., Ampofo, B., Zhong, S. &amp; Fei, Z. The epigenome and transcriptional dynamics of fruit ripening. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 68, 61–84 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1958">58</a></sup>, such transition of allele dominance for <i>Mdg_10g010160</i>, as well as 111 other genes with a similar dominance pattern, may indicate an allele-level interplay that regulates apple fruit development and ripening.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>Unlike many annual crops, apple domestication was mainly driven by hybridization of different wild species. Traits introgressed in the hybrid are often not fixed and could be lost when propagated by seeds. Understanding of the molecular basis of trait variability, which requires the knowledge of the diploid alleles, is critical for fixation of desirable traits in apple breeding. We generated reference quality and haplotype-resolved diploid genomes of Gala, a top apple cultivar in the world, frequently used as a parental line in apple breeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Norelli, J. L. et al. Genotyping-by-sequencing markers facilitate the identification of quantitative trait loci controlling resistance to Penicillium expansum in Malus sieversii. PLoS ONE 12, e0172949 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1975">40</a></sup>, and the two major wild progenitors, <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i>. The two wild progenitors showed distinct demographic histories, with <i>M. sieversii</i> recovering its population size after the first bottleneck whereas populations of <i>M. sylvestris</i> continued to decline, explaining why <i>M. sylvestris</i> has been considered as an endangered species in Europe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Jacques, D., Vandermijnsbrugge, K., Lemaire, S., Antofie, A. &amp; Lateur, M. Natural distribution and variability of wild apple (Malus sylvestris) in Belgium. Belg. J. Bot. 142, 39–49 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1995">59</a></sup>. At least 28% of syntenic genome regions in Gala, GDDH13 and HFTF1, and 23% of the Gala genome harbor alleles derived from different progenitors. Such high level of heterozygosity may have a twofold impact on the genome: (1) masking the deleterious effect of recessive alleles, which can create heterosis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Schnable, P. S. &amp; Springer, N. M. Progress toward understanding heterosis in crop plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 64, 71–88 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d38521e1999">10</a></sup>; and (2) causing ASE. We found at least 19% of Gala genes showed ASE during its fruit development, and these genes were involved in diverse biological processes related to fruit quality. It is worth noting that the role of ASE in apple domestication has long been ignored, probably limited by the lack of diploid phased and wild progenitor genome sequences. Hence, the high-resolution fruit ASE analysis in the present study filled an important gap in our understanding of fruit trait regulation and domestication of cultivated apples.</p><p>Fruit weight, acidity, sweetness, firmness and polyphenol content are well-known domestication traits that remain as the main targets of current apple breeding programs. Our results unraveled many previously unreported trait-associated genes that are located at selective sweeps. Analysis of the origin of these genes, as well as others with established roles, provided new insight into the evolution of trait-associated alleles during apple domestication. Moreover, we found that numerous trait-associated alleles have been fixed or nearly fixed in the cultivated apple and one of the wild progenitors. Such a pattern is different from that in many other crops, for which major domestication trait-associated alleles are fixed only in the cultivars. This demonstrates the uniqueness of apple domestication that was mainly driven by hybridization of different wild species, rather than by evolution to form an independent lineage or species.</p><p>Pan-genomes complement reference genomes by including nonreference genes present in a population. A total of 1,736–3,438 new genes were discovered in the 3 <i>Malus</i> populations. The observation of 12.7–18.7% of genes in the pan-genomes showing PAVs highlights the genetic plasticity in apple populations. Unlike annual crops such as the tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Gao, L. et al. The tomato pan-genome uncovers new genes and a rare allele regulating fruit flavor. Nat. Genet. 51, 1044–1051 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2012">13</a></sup>, the pan-genome size of the cultivated apple is larger than that of wild progenitors, possibly due to the outcrossing nature and extensive introgression from wild species. This distinctive feature suggests that introgression of new genes/alleles is possibly a hallmark of crops domesticated through hybridization. PAV analysis identified hundreds of genes that were uniquely favored in the two wild progenitors, which were further preserved in the cultivated population. These genes, along with the identified new genes, provide useful resources for understanding the genetic basis of various apple traits and for facilitating future apple breeding.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Library construction and sequencing</h3><p>For de novo assemblies, young leaves from plants of <i>M. domestica</i> cv. Gala (PI 392303), <i>M. sieversii</i> (PI 613981) and <i>M. sylvestris</i> (PI 633825), grown at the apple germplasm repository of the Plant Genetic Resources Unit, USDA-ARS, were collected and used for DNA extraction with the DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (QIAGEN). PCR-free paired-end and mate-pair libraries were constructed using the Illumina Genomic DNA Sample Preparation kit and the Nextera Mate Pair Sample Preparation kit, respectively, following the manufacturer’s instructions (Illumina). All libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 4000 system with the paired-end mode. In addition, high-molecular-weight DNA was prepared and used to construct linked read libraries using the 10x Genomics Chromium System and PacBio SMRTbell libraries using SMRTbell Express Template Prep Kit 2.0, following the manufacturers’ protocols. The 10x Genomics libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq X system with the paired-end mode. SMRTbell libraries were sequenced on a PacBio Sequel II system and consensus reads (HiFi reads) were generated using ccs software (<a href="https://github.com/pacificbiosciences/unanimity">https://github.com/pacificbiosciences/unanimity</a>) with the parameter ‘-minPasses 3’. For genome resequencing, sample preparation and library construction and sequencing were conducted as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Duan, N. et al. Genome re-sequencing reveals the history of apple and supports a two-stage model for fruit enlargement. Nat. Commun. 8, 249 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2044">20</a></sup>.</p><p>To assist gene prediction, RNA-sequencing (RNA-seq) data were generated from samples of fruit flesh, peel, flowers and leaves for each accession, and also from fruit samples of Gala collected at 11, 17, 29, 36, 43, 50, 57, 64, 71, 85, 99, 113 and 127 d.a.f., respectively, with three biological replicates except for 11 d.a.f., which had two. Total RNA was extracted using the RNeasy mini kit (QIAGEN). Strand-specific RNA-seq libraries were prepared using the protocol described in Zhong et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Zhong, S. et al. High-throughput Illumina strand-specific RNA sequencing library preparation. Cold Spring Harb. Protoc. 2011, 940–949 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2051">60</a></sup> and sequenced on a HiSeq 4000 system using the paired-end mode.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome assembly</h3><p>Genomes of the three accessions were first assembled with DeNovoMAGIC3 (NRGene) using Illumina paired-end and mate-pair, and 10x Genomics reads. The algorithm of the software was elaborated elsewhere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93–97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2063">61</a></sup>. For each accession, a phased diploid assembly, which comprised scaffolds each of which originated from a single (phased) allele, and an unphased consensus assembly, which comprised scaffolds containing collapsed sequences from the two alleles of each genome locus, were generated.</p><p>A pipeline was developed to validate and improve the assemblies based on the mapping of mate-pair reads and collinearity with the high-quality GDDH13 genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099–1106 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2070">9</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">1</a>). For each accession, first, the mate-pair reads were aligned to the assembly using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2077">62</a></sup> (v.0.7.16a-r1181) to derive physical coverage of the assembly. Gaps at which the physical coverage was less than half of that in the 2-kb flanking regions or &lt;20 were recorded. Second, we evaluated the reliability of connecting the two scaffolds at each recorded gap through: (1) aligning the 2-kb flanking sequences to the GDDH13 genome—the connection was considered valid if the two sequences located within a 100-kb window on the GDDH13 genome; and (2) anchoring the two scaffolds to high-density genetic maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Di Pierro, E. A. et al. A high-density, multi-parental SNP genetic map on apple validates a new mapping approach for outcrossing species. Hortic. Res. 3, 16057 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2081">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Howard, N. P. et al. Elucidation of the ‘Honeycrisp’ pedigree through haplotype analysis with a multi-family integrated SNP linkage map and a large apple (Malus × domestica) pedigree-connected SNP data set. Hortic. Res. 4, 17003 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2084">27</a></sup>. The connected scaffolds consistent with the genetic maps were considered valid. Scaffolds with invalid connections were broken. Last, the resulting final scaffolds were re-anchored using ALLMAPS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Tang, H. et al. ALLMAPS: robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps. Genome Biol. 16, 3 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2088">63</a></sup> with the genetic maps and syntenic information with the GDDH13 genome. Additional steps were taken to validate scaffolding of phased diploid assemblies, because the existence of both alleles would challenge the assignment of mate-pair reads. Therefore, we first performed all-versus-all BLAST (identity &gt;95%) of each of the three diploid assemblies. Scaffolds contained by or overlapped (&gt;50%) with others were separated and formed a collection of alternative alleles. Each collection (comprising primary or alternative alleles) was validated separately and re-anchored into pseudomolecules using the procedure described above.</p><p>To further validate and improve the assemblies, we generated 37–81× PacBio HiFi reads for the three accessions. These long (~12 kb) and highly accurate (&gt;99%) HiFi reads were assembled using Hifiasm (<a href="https://github.com/chhylp123/hifiasm">https://github.com/chhylp123/hifiasm</a>) and HiCanu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Nurk, S. et al. HiCanu: accurate assembly of segmental duplications, satellites, and allelic variants from high-fidelity long reads. Genome Res. 30, 1291–1305 (2020)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2102">64</a></sup>, respectively. Two independent pipelines were developed to improve the quality of DeNovoMAGIC haploid and diploid genomes with the assemblies from HiFi reads (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">1</a>). For each haploid genome, we first aligned the Hifiasm-assembled contigs to the DeNovoMAGIC haploid genome using MUMMER4 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Marçais, G. et al. MUMmer4: a fast and versatile genome alignment system. PLoS Comput. Biol. 14, e1005944 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2109">65</a></sup>, and the reciprocal best alignments were identified using a delta-filter program (with the parameter ‘-g, -l 10000’) in the MUMMER4 package. Based on the alignments, gaps in the DeNovoMAGIC haploid assembly were filled with sequences from the Hifiasm assembly. The gap-filled haploid assembly was further processed to remove redundant scaffolds (coverage &gt;0.9 and identity &gt;95%). The final haploid assembly was then used for the second round of improvement with the HiCanu assembly, using the same procedure described above. For each diploid genome, the Hifiasm-assembled unitigs were aligned to the DeNovoMAGIC diploid scaffolds with MUMMER4. The reciprocal best alignments with size &gt;5 kb and identity &gt;99.5% were identified. Gap regions in the diploid scaffolds were filled with corresponding sequences of Hifiasm unitigs based on alignments spanning the gaps. Diploid scaffolds aligned to the same unitig with proper order were joined together and the redundant scaffolds (coverage &gt;0.9 and identity &gt;99.5%) were removed. The final diploid scaffolds were used for the second round of improvement with the HiCanu assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Haplome construction</h3><p>To construct the two haplomes (haploid genomes) for each accession, we first aligned the diploid assembly against the GDDH13 genome using NUCmer in MUMMER4 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Marçais, G. et al. MUMmer4: a fast and versatile genome alignment system. PLoS Comput. Biol. 14, e1005944 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2121">65</a></sup>) with default parameters. Each scaffold of the assembly was assigned to one specific chromosome according to the alignments. All-versus-all BLAST was applied to scaffolds assigned to the same chromosome, and allelic scaffolds that were contained by others were temporarily removed. The remaining scaffolds were aligned to the corresponding chromosome sequences of the GDDH13 genome, and the resulting alignments were filtered by the delta-filter program and subjected to scaffold anchoring using show-tiling in MUMMER4. As the allele redundancy could cause difficulty in scaffold selection at each locus, we used an iterative anchoring approach with manual examination to avoid/minimize the inclusion of (partial) redundant alleles, while keeping the adjacent scaffolds at minimal distance. For each chromosome, we started with the longest scaffold and preserved others that were anchored properly with the longest scaffold. This could take several rounds to include or exclude scaffolds to produce the first haplome (haplome A). Scaffolds not included by haplome A were used for generation of the second haplome (haplome B). Scaffolds assigned to neither haplome A nor haplome B were collectively referred to as unplaced scaffolds. Scaffolds in each of the haplome were used to construct homologous chromosomes with the high-density genetic maps and genomic synteny as described above.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Repeat annotation and gene prediction</h3><p>Repeat library for each apple accession was built using EDTA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Ou, S. et al. Benchmarking transposable element annotation methods for creation of a streamlined, comprehensive pipeline. Genome Biol. 20, 275 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2133">66</a></sup>, a de novo transposable element (TE) annotator that integrates structure- and homology-based approaches for TE identification. Protein-coding genes were predicted from repeat-masked assemblies using MAKER-P<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2137">67</a></sup> (v.2.31.10), which integrates evidence from protein homology, transcripts and ab initio predictions. RNA-seq reads were processed to remove adapters and low-quality bases using Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2141">68</a></sup> (v.0.35), and assembled both de novo and genome guided using Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Haas, B. J. et al. De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis. Nat. Protoc. 8, 1494–1512 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2145">69</a></sup> (v.2.4.0). The resulting assembled contigs were used as the transcript evidence. Proteomes of published apple genomes as well as closely related species, including pear, peach and strawberry, were aligned to the assemblies using Exonerate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Slater, G. S. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinf. 6, 31 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2149">70</a></sup> to provide homology-based evidence. Finally, ab initio gene predictions from SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinf. 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2154">71</a></sup>, AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435–W439 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2158">72</a></sup> (v.3.3) and GeneMark-ES<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Lomsadze, A., Ter-Hovhannisyan, V., Chernoff, Y. O. &amp; Borodovsky, M. Gene identification in novel eukaryotic genomes by self-training algorithm. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 6494–6506 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2162">73</a></sup> (v.4.35) were integrated with transcript and protein-homology evidence by MAKER-P to generate the final gene models. Protein sequences of the predicted genes were compared against GenBank nonredundant protein (nr) and InterPro databases to identify homology information and protein domains, respectively. Gene Ontology annotation was done using Blast2GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674–3676 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2166">74</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genetic contribution of wild progenitors to the cultivated apple</h3><p>A whole genome alignment for each pair of assemblies was performed with NUCmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Marçais, G. et al. MUMmer4: a fast and versatile genome alignment system. PLoS Comput. Biol. 14, e1005944 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2179">65</a></sup>. To infer genetic contributions of the two wild progenitors to the cultivated apple, we projected all pairwise genome alignments to the Gala haploid consensus genome, and calculated genetic divergence using distmat (<a href="http://www.bioinformatics.nl/cgi-bin/emboss/distmat">http://www.bioinformatics.nl/cgi-bin/emboss/distmat</a>) with the Jukes–Cantor correction for each nonoverlapping 50-kb window on the Gala genome. The window-based genetic divergences were fitted with local regression to show the pattern of relative contribution of the two wild progenitors. To quantify the contributions, we evaluated genetic divergences in all windows with alignment length &gt;100 bp. The mean of the smaller divergences of all windows was 0.8% (± 0.8%), whereas the mean of the larger divergences was 1.4% (± 1.2%). Accordingly, we assigned the genetic origin of each window in the cultivated apple to either <i>M. sieversii</i> or <i>M. sylvestris</i>, whichever had the smaller divergence value to the cultivated apple genomes, which must be &lt;1.4%. Meantime, we also required that the difference between the two divergence values (<i>M. sieversii</i> to the cultivated apple and <i>M. sylvestris</i> to the cultivated apple) must be &gt;0.2%, which was the lower bound of the mean difference across all windows (1.3% ± 1.1%). Windows that did not meet the criteria were classified as those with unclear genetic origin. We used variable window sizes (5, 10, 20, 30, 40, 50, 60 and 70 kb) and minimal alignment length cutoffs (100, 300, 500 and 1,000 bp) for the analysis, and obtained similar results.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Comparative genomics and divergence time estimation</h3><p>Orthologous groups of selected plant species were constructed using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2211">75</a></sup>. Protein sequences of each single-copy orthologous group were aligned with MAFFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Katoh, K., Misawa, K., Kuma, K. I. &amp; Miyata, T. MAFFT: a novel method for rapid multiple sequence alignment based on fast Fourier transform. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 3059–3066 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2215">76</a></sup> (v.7.313) with parameters ‘--maxiterate 1000 --localpair’. Each alignment was used to build a maximum likelihood phylogeny with IQ-TREE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Nguyen, L.-T., Schmidt, H. A., von Haeseler, A. &amp; Minh, B. Q. IQ-TREE: a fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 268–274 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2219">77</a></sup>. We then compared each gene tree with the species tree and preserved orthologous groups with gene trees that were consistent with the species tree. Alignments of resulting orthologous groups were concatenated to build a maximum likelihood phylogeny. The divergence time was estimated using MCMCTree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Yang, Z. &amp; Rannala, B. Bayesian estimation of species divergence times under a molecular clock using multiple fossil calibrations with soft bounds. Mol. Biol. Evol. 23, 212–226 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2223">78</a></sup> with branch lengths estimated by BASEML in the PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2227">79</a></sup> and independent rate model for time estimation. We constrained the root age to &lt;200 Ma and the Rosids age to 128.63 to 85.8 Ma (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Clarke, J. T., Warnock, R. C. &amp; Donoghue, P. C. Establishing a time‐scale for plant evolution. N. Phytol. 192, 266–301 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2232">80</a></sup>), and performed 20,000 samplings with ‘burnin=20000’ and ‘sampfreq=500’.</p><p>The apple mutation rate was inferred using the formula <i>µ</i> = <i>D</i>/2<i>T</i>, where <i>D</i> is the evolutionary distance of <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i> (0.014; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM1">6b</a>) and <i>T</i> is the divergence time of the two species (1.8 Ma; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#Fig1">1b</a>). The mutation rate was estimated to be 3.9 × 10<sup>−9</sup> substitutions per site per year, which is close to a previous estimation of 4 × 10<sup>−9</sup> for apples based on a small-scale dataset<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Cornille, A. et al. New insight into the history of domesticated apple: secondary contribution of the European wild apple to the genome of cultivated varieties. PLoS Genet. 8, e1002703 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2272">19</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Variant calling and population genetic analyses</h3><p>Illumina paired-end reads were processed to remove adapters and low-quality sequences using Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2284">68</a></sup>. Cleaned reads were mapped to the apple reference genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Zhang, L. et al. A high-quality apple genome assembly reveals the association of a retrotransposon and red fruit colour. Nat. Commun. 10, 1494 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2288">23</a></sup> using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2292">62</a></sup>. The resulting alignments were processed with Picard (<a href="https://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/">https://broadinstitute.github.io/picard</a>) to keep those uniquely aligned to the genome and having mapping quality &gt;20. Variants were called using BCFtools (<a href="http://samtools.github.io/bcftools/">http://samtools.github.io/bcftools</a>) and filtered using VCFtools (<a href="http://vcftools.sourceforge.net/">http://vcftools.sourceforge.net</a>) with parameters ‘-maf 0.05 -minQ 30 -max-missing 0.9’. A neighbor-joining phylogeny was constructed based on the <i>P</i> distance matrix calculated by VCF2Dis (<a href="https://github.com/BGI-shenzhen/VCF2Dis">https://github.com/BGI-shenzhen/VCF2Dis</a>), and the principal component analysis was performed with EIGENSOFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Patterson, N., Price, A. L. &amp; Reich, D. Population structure and eigenanalysis. PLoS Genet. 2, e190 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2328">81</a></sup>. The population structure was analyzed using the STRUCTURE program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Hubisz, M. J., Falush, D., Stephens, M. &amp; Pritchard, J. K. Inferring weak population structure with the assistance of sample group information. Mol. Ecol. Resour. 9, 1322–1332 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2332">82</a></sup>. The likelihood of <i>K</i> from 1 to 11 was estimated using 20,000 randomly selected SNPs. Each <i>K</i> was run 20 times and the number of kinships suggesting the most likely number of clusters in the population was obtained. Seven <i>M. domestica</i> accessions (A12, R05, R06, R08, R11, R12 and M27) and one <i>M. sieversii</i> accession (C84) were excluded from the downstream analyses due to their suspicious taxonomy classifications according to our phylogeny and genomic origin analyses. Genome-wide scan of selective sweeps was performed by comparing allele frequency between <i>M. domestica</i> and <i>M. sieversii</i> and between <i>M. domestica</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i> using XP-CLR with the parameters ‘-w1 0.005 200 1000 -p0 0.95’. Nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) and Tajima’s <i>D</i> were calculated based on a sliding window of 20 kb and a step size of 1 kb. The population-level SVs were identified using LUMPY<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Layer, R. M., Chiang, C., Quinlan, A. R. &amp; Hall, I. M. LUMPY: a probabilistic framework for structural variant discovery. Genome Biol. 15, R84 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2368">83</a></sup> and SVs between haplomes were identified using Assemblytics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Nattestad, M. &amp; Schatz, M. C. Assemblytics: a web analytics tool for the detection of variants from an assembly. Bioinformatics 32, 3021–3023 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2372">84</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Inference of demographic history</h3><p>The dynamics of historic population size was inferred using the pairwise sequentially Markovian coalescent (PSMC) model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Inference of human population history from individual whole-genome sequences. Nature 475, 493–496 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2384">85</a></sup>. Reads of selected samples were mapped to the reference genome using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93–97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2388">61</a></sup>, and based on the alignments of heterozygous sites that were identified using SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2392">86</a></sup>. The psmc program in the PSMC package was used to estimate the effective population size with parameters ‘-N25 -t15 -r5 -p ‘4 + 25*2 + 4 + 6’’. The estimated apple mutation rate (3.9 × 10<sup>−9</sup>) and the average generation time<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Cornille, A. et al. New insight into the history of domesticated apple: secondary contribution of the European wild apple to the genome of cultivated varieties. PLoS Genet. 8, e1002703 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2398">19</a></sup> (7.5 years) were used to scale the population parameters into years and individuals.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Pan-genome construction and analysis</h3><p>To build pan-genomes, cleaned reads of 36 <i>M. domestica</i>, 36 <i>M. sieversii</i> and 11 <i>M. sylvesitris</i> accessions were assembled de novo using SPAdes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Bankevich, A. et al. SPAdes: a new genome assembly algorithm and its applications to single-cell sequencing. J. Comput Biol. 19, 455–477 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2419">87</a></sup> (v.3.13.0). The assembled contigs of <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i> accessions were aligned to the corresponding reference genomes (haploid consensus assemblies) using QUAST-LG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Mikheenko, A., Prjibelski, A., Saveliev, V., Antipov, D. &amp; Gurevich, A. Versatile genome assembly evaluation with QUAST-LG. Bioinformatics 34, i142–i150 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2430">88</a></sup>. Contigs containing unaligned segment(s) (identity cutoff 90%) with single-segment length &gt;500 bp were retained. The resulting contigs, probably from nonplant organisms or mitochondrial/chloroplast genomes, were removed. The contigs were further processed with CD-HIT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Fu, L., Niu, B., Zhu, Z., Wu, S. &amp; Li, W. CD-HIT: accelerated for clustering the next-generation sequencing data. Bioinformatics 28, 3150–3152 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2434">89</a></sup> to remove redundancies with a 90% identity cutoff. For <i>M. domestica</i>, first, genes from the four previously published genome assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, X. et al. Improved hybrid de novo genome assembly of domesticated apple (Malus × domestica). GigaScience 5, 35 (2016)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2441">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2441_1">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Zhang, L. et al. A high-quality apple genome assembly reveals the association of a retrotransposon and red fruit colour. Nat. Commun. 10, 1494 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2444">23</a></sup> were aligned to the Gala genome to identify high-confidence genes that were not present in Gala (identity &lt;90% or coverage &lt;50%). Then, nonreference sequences of resequencing accessions were identified using the same strategy for <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i>, except that contigs were aligned to all of the five <i>M. domestica</i> genome assemblies. Protein-coding genes were predicted from the nonredundant unaligned contigs using MAKER-P<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2458">67</a></sup> as described above.</p><p>PAVs of protein-coding genes in the pan-genome for each species were identified using the same method described in Gao et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Gao, L. et al. The tomato pan-genome uncovers new genes and a rare allele regulating fruit flavor. Nat. Genet. 51, 1044–1051 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2465">13</a></sup>. A gene was defined as absent in a given accession when &lt;20% of its coding region was covered by at least two reads. To investigate cross-species PAV patterns, we built orthologue groups of the three pan-genomes. We first used MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2469">90</a></sup> to identify orthologues based on genome synteny. Genes that failed to be classified by MCScanX were grouped using BLASTp following the bidirectional best-hit strategy. Singletons as well as orthologue groups with missing genes in certain species were further checked by aligning their CDS sequences against the genomes using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT—the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2473">91</a></sup>. For the PAV of an orthologue group in each accession, we defined it as absent in a given accession if none of the orthologues within the group passed the PAV criterion when reads were mapped to the corresponding pan-genomes. The Fisher’s exact test followed by multiple test correction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Benjamini, Y. &amp; Hochberg, Y. Controlling the false discovery rate: a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J. R. Stat. Soc. Ser. B 57, 289–300 (1995)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2477">92</a></sup> was used to identify favorable genes in different species (adjusted <i>P</i> value &lt;0.05).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">ASE</h3><p>Cleaned RNA-seq reads were first mapped against both haplomes of Gala using STAR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Dobin, A. et al. STAR: ultrafast universal RNA-seq aligner. Bioinformatics 29, 15–21 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2492">93</a></sup> (v.2.7.3a). Reads uniquely aligned to the same chromosome in both haplomes were preserved and assigned to the allele if the alignment had a higher score and fewer mismatches than to the other allele. Reads that failed to be assigned were mainly from homozygous genomic region or regions that were unassembled or haplotype unresolved. The latter was addressed through SNP analysis. We called heterozygous SNPs in the Gala consensus genome using Illumina paired-end reads with GATK4 (<a href="https://gatk.broadinstitute.org">https://gatk.broadinstitute.org</a>). Biallelic SNPs that passed hard filtering using GATK (‘QD &lt; 2, QUAL &lt; 60, SOR &gt; 3, FS &gt; 60, MQ &lt; 40, MQRankSum &lt; −12.5, ReadPosRankSum &lt; −8’) and supported by at least five reads for both reference and alternative alleles were kept, and phased based on the two haplomes. For each SNP site, when genotype information was available for only one haplome, the genotype of another haplome was imputed based on the SNP calls. With the phased SNPs, we further classified the unseparated RNA-seq reads into different alleles using SNPsplit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Krueger, F. &amp; Andrews, S. R. SNPsplit: allele-specific splitting of alignments between genomes with known SNP genotypes. F1000 Research 5, 1479 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2503">94</a></sup>. Reads separated by the above two approaches were combined for downstream ASE analysis. Unlike read assignment biased toward the reference allele in conventional ASE studies, for which only a consensus genome was used as a reference<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Castel, S. E., Levy-Moonshine, A., Mohammadi, P., Banks, E. &amp; Lappalainen, T. Tools and best practices for data processing in allelic expression analysis. Genome Biol. 16, 195 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2507">95</a></sup>, in the present study the ratio of reads assigned to either haplome was close to 1 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM3">13</a>), indicating unbiased read assignment. The allele-specific reads were mapped to the Gala consensus genome using STAR and genes with total counts &gt;10 in all biological replicates were analyzed for ASE using DESeq2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Love, M. I., Huber, W. &amp; Anders, S. Moderated estimation of fold change and dispersion for RNA-seq data with DESeq2. Genome Biol. 15, 550 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d38521e2515">96</a></sup>). A gene was considered to have ASE if the expression difference of the two alleles was significantly greater than twofold (adjusted <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Genome assemblies, raw genome and transcriptome sequencing reads have been deposited in the National Center for Biotechnology Information BioProject database (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject</a>) under the accession no. <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA591623">PRJNA591623</a>. Genome assemblies and annotated genes, nonreference genome sequences and annotated genes of the apple pan-genomes, and SNPs and SVs called from the genome resequencing data are also freely available at <a href="http://bioinfo.bti.cornell.edu/apple_genome">http://bioinfo.bti.cornell.edu/apple_genome</a>.</p>
            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="code-availability" data-title="Code availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="code-availability-content">
              
              <p>Computational pipelines related to assembly validation and improvement can be accessed through <a href="https://github.com/XuepengSun/apple_diploid_genomes">https://github.com/XuepengSun/apple_diploid_genomes</a>.</p>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 7 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=The%20evolutionary%20significance%20of%20polyploidy&amp;journal=Nat.%20Rev.%20Genet.&amp;volume=18&amp;pages=411-424&amp;publication_year=2017&amp;author=Peer%2CY&amp;author=Mizrachi%2CE&amp;author=Marchal%2CK">

                </p></li><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="S. Wu, " /><meta itemprop="datePublished" content="2018" /><meta itemprop="headline" content="Wu, S. et al. Colors mark different copies of 27-mers in the genome assembly. The black area measures the proportion of 27-mers that were present in the sequencing reads but missing in the assemblies, which could be sequencing errors and contaminated sequences from organelles and microorganisms. The presence of a small portion of single-copy k-mer showing homozygous peak in the diploid assemblies could be due to: 1) the two alleles are nearly identical and were collapsed into one copy in the diploid assembly; and 2) the phased diploid assemblies are incomplete, with only one allele assembled for some loci.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig8"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9/figures/8" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-00723-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_723_Fig8_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 2 BUSCO evaluation of apple genome assemblies.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Gene space coverage of apple genome assemblies was assessed using 1,614 core conserved plant genes with BUSCO (<a href="https://busco.ezlab.org/">https://busco.ezlab.org/</a>).</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig9"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9/figures/9" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-00723-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_723_Fig9_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 3 A mis-assembly identified in the genomes of GDDH13 and HFTH1.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Synteny of chromosome 1 between <i>Malus sylvestris</i>, Gala, GDDH13 and HFTH1. <b>b</b> and <b>c</b>, Alignments of PacBio long reads from GDDH13 and HFTH1 to the corresponding genome assemblies indicated that the ~5-Mb inversion on chromosome 1 in GDDH13 and HFTH1 is a mis-assembly in both genomes. Breakpoints (indicated by red arrows) of the inversion in both GDDH13 and HFTH1 assemblies were surrounded by regions without PacBio long read support. <b>d</b>, Breakpoints (indicated by red arrows) of the inversion in Gala and <i>M. sylvestris</i> were well supported by both Illumina and PacBio HiFi reads. <b>e</b>, Genetic maps show inconsistency in the ~5-Mb region in both GDDH13 and HFTH1 assemblies.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig10"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-00723-9/figures/10" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-00723-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_723_Fig10_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 4 LTR-RT diversity in cultivated and wild apples.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Comparison of LTR-RT insertions in different assemblies. Genomic sequences harboring LTR-RTs in <i>M. sieversii</i> or <i>M. sylvestris</i> were compared to the syntenic regions of other <i>Malus</i> assemblies to check the presence or absence of LTR-RT insertion in same regions. The percentage of unique LTR-RT insertion was calculated by dividing the number of wild species-specific LTR-RT insertions over the total number of traceable LTR-RTs in the target genome. <b>b</b>, Venn diagram showing percentages of positionally conserved LTR-RT insertions in the three cultivated apple genomes. <b>c</b>, Inheritance of the <i>MYB1</i> (Mdg_09g022880) locus in the cultivated apples and its contribution to apple fruit skin color. TSD, target site duplication.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig11"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title Only top 50 categories are shown. A, the dominant allele was from haplome A. B, the dominant allele was from haplome B. DAF, days after full bloom.</p></div></div></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec24" data-title="Supplementary information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec24-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec24">Supplementary information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec24-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info">
